<p><b>Stan Lee</b><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> (born <b>Stanley Martin Lieber</b>, December 28, 1922) is an American comic-book writer, editor, publisher, media producer, television host, actor, and former president and chairman of <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">Marvel Comics</a>. In collaboration with several artists, including <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kirby" title="Jack Kirby">Jack Kirby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steve_Ditko" title="Steve Ditko">Steve Ditko</a>, he created <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man" title="Spider-Man">Spider-Man</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hulk_(comics)" title="Hulk (comics)">Hulk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Strange" title="Doctor Strange">Doctor Strange</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four" title="Fantastic Four">Fantastic Four</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron_Man" title="Iron Man">Iron Man</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daredevil_(Marvel_Comics_character)" title="Daredevil (Marvel Comics character)">Daredevil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thor_(Marvel_Comics)" title="Thor (Marvel Comics)">Thor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/X-Men" title="X-Men">X-Men</a>, and many other fictional <a href="/wiki/Character_(arts)" title="Character (arts)">characters</a>, introducing a thoroughly <a href="/wiki/Shared_universe" title="Shared universe">shared universe</a> into <a href="/wiki/Superhero" title="Superhero">superhero</a> comic books. In addition, he challenged the comics' industry's censorship organization, the <a href="/wiki/Comics_Code_Authority" title="Comics Code Authority">Comics Code Authority</a>, forcing it to reform its policies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources in the body of the article. (October 2016)">not verified in body</span></a></i>]</sup> Lee subsequently led the expansion of Marvel Comics from a small division of a publishing house to a large <a href="/wiki/Multimedia" title="Multimedia">multimedia</a> corporation.</p>
<p>He was inducted into the comic book industry's <a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner_Award_Hall_of_Fame" class="mw-redirect" title="Will Eisner Award Hall of Fame">Will Eisner Award Hall of Fame</a> in 1994 and the <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kirby_Hall_of_Fame" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack Kirby Hall of Fame">Jack Kirby Hall of Fame</a> in 1995. Lee received a <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Arts" class="mw-redirect" title="National Medal of Arts">National Medal of Arts</a> in 2008.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<p>Stanley Martin Lieber was born on December 28, 1922 in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, U.S.,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> in the apartment of his <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Romania" title="History of the Jews in Romania">Romanian-born Jewish</a> immigrant parents, Celia (née Solomon) and Jack Lieber, at the corner of West 98th Street and <a href="/wiki/West_End_Avenue" class="mw-redirect" title="West End Avenue">West End Avenue</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autobio-p5_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autobio-p5-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> His father, trained as a dress cutter, worked only sporadically after the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-autobio-p5_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autobio-p5-3">[3]</a></sup> and the family moved further uptown to <a href="/wiki/Fort_Washington_Avenue_(Manhattan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fort Washington Avenue (Manhattan)">Fort Washington Avenue</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Washington_Heights,_Manhattan" title="Washington Heights, Manhattan">Washington Heights, Manhattan</a>. When Lee was nearly 9, his only sibling, brother <a href="/wiki/Larry_Lieber" title="Larry Lieber">Larry Lieber</a>, was born.<sup id="cite_ref-fam_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fam-6">[6]</a></sup> He said in 2006 that as a child he was influenced by books and movies, particularly those with <a href="/wiki/Errol_Flynn" title="Errol Flynn">Errol Flynn</a> playing heroic roles.<sup id="cite_ref-kugel_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kugel-7">[7]</a></sup> By the time Lee was in his teens, the family was living in a one-bedroom apartment at 1720 University Avenue in <a href="/wiki/The_Bronx" title="The Bronx">The Bronx</a>. Lee has described it as "a third-floor apartment facing out back", with he and his brother sharing a bedroom and his parents using a foldout couch.<sup id="cite_ref-fam_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fam-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lee attended <a href="/wiki/DeWitt_Clinton_High_School" title="DeWitt Clinton High School">DeWitt Clinton High School</a> in the Bronx.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In his youth, Lee enjoyed writing, and entertained dreams of one day writing the "<a href="/wiki/Great_American_Novel" title="Great American Novel">Great American Novel</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> He has said that in his youth he worked such part-time jobs as writing <a href="/wiki/Obituary" title="Obituary">obituaries</a> for a <a href="/wiki/News_agency" title="News agency">news service</a> and <a href="/wiki/Press_releases" class="mw-redirect" title="Press releases">press releases</a> for the National <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">Tuberculosis</a> Center;<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> delivering sandwiches for the Jack May pharmacy to offices in <a href="/wiki/Rockefeller_Center" title="Rockefeller Center">Rockefeller Center</a>; working as an office boy for a trouser manufacturer; ushering at the Rivoli Theater on <a href="/wiki/Broadway_theater" class="mw-redirect" title="Broadway theater">Broadway</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> and selling subscriptions to the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Herald_Tribune" title="New York Herald Tribune">New York Herald Tribune</a></i> newspaper.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> He graduated from high school early, aged 16½ in 1939, and joined the <a href="/wiki/Works_Progress_Administration" title="Works Progress Administration">WPA</a> <a href="/wiki/Federal_Theatre_Project" title="Federal Theatre Project">Federal Theatre Project</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career">Early career</span></h3>
<p>With the help of his uncle Robbie Solomon,<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Lee became an assistant in 1939 at the new <a href="/wiki/Timely_Comics" title="Timely Comics">Timely Comics</a> division of <a href="/wiki/Pulp_magazine" title="Pulp magazine">pulp magazine</a> and comic-book publisher <a href="/wiki/Martin_Goodman_(publisher)" title="Martin Goodman (publisher)">Martin Goodman</a>'s company. Timely, by the 1960s, would evolve into Marvel Comics. Lee, whose cousin Jean<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> was Goodman's wife, was formally hired by Timely editor <a href="/wiki/Joe_Simon" title="Joe Simon">Joe Simon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[n 1]</a></sup></p>
<p>His duties were prosaic at first. "In those days [the artists] dipped the pen in ink, [so] I had to make sure the inkwells were filled", Lee recalled in 2009. "I went down and got them their lunch, I did proofreading, I erased the pencils from the finished pages for them".<sup id="cite_ref-laxgb_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-laxgb-18">[17]</a></sup> Marshaling his childhood ambition to be a writer, young Stanley Lieber made his comic-book debut with the text filler "<a href="/wiki/Captain_America" title="Captain America">Captain America</a> Foils the Traitor's Revenge" in <i>Captain America Comics</i> #3 (May 1941), using the pseudonym Stan Lee,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> which years later he would adopt as his legal name. Lee later explained in his autobiography and numerous other sources that he had intended to save his given name for more literary work. This initial story also introduced Captain America's trademark ricocheting shield-toss, which immediately became one of the character's signatures.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11</sup></p>
<p>He graduated from writing filler to actual comics with a backup feature, "'Headline' Hunter, Foreign Correspondent", two issues later. Lee's first superhero co-creation was the <a href="/wiki/Destroyer_(Timely_Comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Destroyer (Timely Comics)">Destroyer</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/Mystic_Comics" title="Mystic Comics">Mystic Comics</a></i> #6 (August 1941). Other characters he created during this period fans and historians call the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_comics" class="mw-redirect" title="Golden Age of comics">Golden Age of comics</a> include <a href="/wiki/Jack_Frost_(Marvel_Comics)" title="Jack Frost (Marvel Comics)">Jack Frost</a>, debuting in <i><a href="/wiki/USA_Comics" class="mw-redirect" title="USA Comics">USA Comics</a></i> #1 (August 1941), and <a href="/wiki/Father_Time_(Marvel_Comics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Father Time (Marvel Comics)">Father Time</a>, debuting in <i>Captain America Comics</i> #6 (August 1941).<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:12–13</sup></p>
<p>When Simon and his creative partner <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kirby" title="Jack Kirby">Jack Kirby</a> left late in 1941, following a dispute with Goodman, the 30-year-old publisher installed Lee, just under 19 years old, as interim editor.<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:14</sup> <sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> The youngster showed a knack for the business that led him to remain as the comic-book division's editor-in-chief, as well as art director for much of that time, until 1972, when he would succeed Goodman as publisher.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EGWC_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EGWC-23">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lee entered the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a> in early 1942 and served in the US in the <a href="/wiki/Signal_Corps_(United_States_Army)" title="Signal Corps (United States Army)">Signal Corps</a>, repairing <a href="/wiki/Telegraph_pole" class="mw-redirect" title="Telegraph pole">telegraph poles</a> and other communications equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-DAK64_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DAK64-24">[23]</a></sup> He was later transferred to the Training Film Division, where he worked writing manuals, <a href="/wiki/Training_film" title="Training film">training films</a>, and slogans, and occasionally <a href="/wiki/Cartoonist" title="Cartoonist">cartooning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> His military classification, he says, was "playwright"; he adds that only nine men in the US Army were given that title.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vincent_Fago" title="Vincent Fago">Vincent Fago</a>, editor of Timely's "animation comics" section, which put out humor and <a href="/wiki/Funny_animal" title="Funny animal">funny animal</a> comics, filled in until Lee returned from his World War II <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">military service</a> in 1945. Lee then lived in the rented top floor of a <a href="/wiki/Brownstone" title="Brownstone">brownstone</a> in the East 90s in Manhattan.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the mid-1950s, by which time the company was now generally known as <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Comics_(1950s)" title="Atlas Comics (1950s)">Atlas Comics</a>, Lee wrote stories in a variety of genres including <a href="/wiki/Romance_comics" title="Romance comics">romance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_comics" title="Western comics">Westerns</a>, humor, science fiction, medieval adventure, <a href="/wiki/Horror_comics" title="Horror comics">horror</a> and suspense. In the 1950s, Lee teamed up with his comic book colleague <a href="/wiki/Dan_DeCarlo" title="Dan DeCarlo">Dan DeCarlo</a> to produce the syndicated newspaper strip, <i>My Friend Irma</i>, based on <a href="/wiki/My_Friend_Irma_(radio-TV)" title="My Friend Irma (radio-TV)">the radio comedy</a> starring <a href="/wiki/Marie_Wilson_(American_actress)" title="Marie Wilson (American actress)">Marie Wilson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> By the end of the decade, Lee had become dissatisfied with his career and considered quitting the field.<sup id="cite_ref-MCBUR_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MCBUR-29">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SLC_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SLC-30">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marvel_revolution">Marvel revolution</span></h3>
<p>In the late 1950s, <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">DC Comics</a> editor <a href="/wiki/Julius_Schwartz" title="Julius Schwartz">Julius Schwartz</a> revived the superhero <a href="/wiki/Archetype" title="Archetype">archetype</a> and experienced a significant success with its updated version of the <a href="/wiki/Flash_(Barry_Allen)" title="Flash (Barry Allen)">Flash</a>, and later with super-team the <a href="/wiki/Justice_League" title="Justice League">Justice League of America</a>. In response, publisher Martin Goodman assigned Lee to create a new superhero team. Lee's wife urged him to experiment with stories he preferred, since he was planning on changing careers and had nothing to lose.<sup id="cite_ref-MCBUR_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MCBUR-29">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SLC_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SLC-30">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lee acted on that advice, giving his superheroes a flawed humanity, a change from the ideal archetypes that were typically written for preteens. Before this, most superheroes were idealistically perfect people with no serious, lasting problems.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> Lee introduced complex, <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(literature)" title="Naturalism (literature)">naturalistic</a> characters<sup id="cite_ref-wrightp207_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wrightp207-32">[31]</a></sup> who could have bad tempers, fits of melancholy, and vanity; they bickered amongst themselves, worried about paying their bills and impressing girlfriends, got bored or even were sometimes physically ill.</p>
<p>The first superhero group Lee and artist Jack Kirby created was the <a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four" title="Fantastic Four">Fantastic Four</a>. The team's immediate popularity<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> led Lee and Marvel's illustrators to produce a cavalcade of new titles. With Kirby primarily, Lee created the <a href="/wiki/Hulk_(comics)" title="Hulk (comics)">Hulk</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thor_(Marvel_Comics)" title="Thor (Marvel Comics)">Thor</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Iron_Man" title="Iron Man">Iron Man</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/X-Men" title="X-Men">X-Men</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Bill_Everett" title="Bill Everett">Bill Everett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daredevil_(Marvel_Comics_character)" title="Daredevil (Marvel Comics character)">Daredevil</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> and with <a href="/wiki/Steve_Ditko" title="Steve Ditko">Steve Ditko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Strange" title="Doctor Strange">Doctor Strange</a><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> and Marvel's most successful character, <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man" title="Spider-Man">Spider-Man</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> all of whom lived in a thoroughly <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Universe" title="Marvel Universe">shared universe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wrightp218_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wrightp218-41">[40]</a></sup> Lee and Kirby gathered several of their newly created characters together into the team title <i>The <a href="/wiki/Avengers_(comics)" title="Avengers (comics)">Avengers</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> and would revive characters from the 1940s such as the <a href="/wiki/Namor" title="Namor">Sub-Mariner</a><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> and Captain America.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Comics historian <a href="/wiki/Peter_Sanderson" title="Peter Sanderson">Peter Sanderson</a> wrote that in the 1960s:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>DC was the equivalent of the big Hollywood studios: After the brilliance of DC's reinvention of the superhero ... in the late 1950s and early 1960s, it had run into a creative drought by the decade's end. There was a new audience for comics now, and it wasn't just the little kids that traditionally had read the books. The Marvel of the 1960s was in its own way the counterpart of the <a href="/wiki/French_New_Wave" title="French New Wave">French New Wave</a>.... Marvel was pioneering new methods of comics storytelling and characterization, addressing more serious themes, and in the process keeping and attracting readers in their teens and beyond. Moreover, among this new generation of readers were people who wanted to write or draw comics themselves, within the new style that Marvel had pioneered, and push the creative envelope still further.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Lee's revolution extended beyond the characters and storylines to the way in which comic books engaged the readership and built a sense of community between fans and creators.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup> He introduced the practice of regularly including a credit panel on the splash page of each story, naming not just the writer and penciller but also the inker and letterer. Regular news about Marvel staff members and upcoming storylines was presented on the <a href="/wiki/Bullpen_Bulletins" title="Bullpen Bulletins">Bullpen Bulletins</a> page, which (like the letter columns that appeared in each title) was written in a friendly, chatty style. Lee has said that his goal was for fans to think of the comics creators as friends, and considered it a mark of his success on this front that, at a time when letters to other comics publishers were typically addressed "Dear Editor", letters to Marvel addressed the creators by first name (e.g. "Dear Stan and Jack").<sup id="cite_ref-DAK64_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DAK64-24">[23]</a></sup> By 1967, the brand was well-enough ensconced in popular culture that a March 3 <a href="/wiki/WBAI" title="WBAI">WBAI</a> radio program with Lee and Kirby as guests was titled "Will Success Spoil Spiderman" [sic].<sup id="cite_ref-nyt03031967_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt03031967-47">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout the 1960s, Lee scripted, art-directed and edited most of Marvel's series, moderated the letters pages, wrote a monthly column called "<a href="/wiki/Stan%27s_Soapbox" class="mw-redirect" title="Stan's Soapbox">Stan's Soapbox</a>", and wrote endless promotional copy, often signing off with his trademark motto, "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/excelsior" class="extiw" title="wikt:excelsior">Excelsior</a>!" (which is also the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_and_territory_mottos" title="List of U.S. state and territory mottos">New York state motto</a>). To maintain his workload and meet deadlines, he used a system that was used previously by various comic-book studios, but due to Lee's success with it, became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Marvel_Method" class="mw-redirect" title="Marvel Method">Marvel Method</a>". Typically, Lee would brainstorm a story with the artist and then prepare a brief synopsis rather than a full script. Based on the synopsis, the artist would fill the allotted number of pages by determining and drawing the panel-to-panel storytelling. After the artist turned in penciled pages, Lee would write the <a href="/wiki/Word_balloon" class="mw-redirect" title="Word balloon">word balloons</a> and captions, and then oversee the lettering and coloring. In effect, the artists were co-plotters, whose collaborative first drafts Lee built upon. Lee recorded messages to the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Merry_Marvel_Marching_Society" title="Merry Marvel Marching Society">Merry Marvel Marching Society</a> fan club in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following Ditko's departure from Marvel in 1966, <a href="/wiki/John_Romita_Sr." title="John Romita Sr.">John Romita Sr.</a> became Lee's collaborator on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Spider-Man" title="The Amazing Spider-Man">The Amazing Spider-Man</a></i>. Within a year, it overtook <i>Fantastic Four</i> to become the company's top seller.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> Lee and Romita's stories focused as much on the social and college lives of the characters as they did on Spider-Man's adventures.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> The stories became more topical, addressing issues such as the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> political elections,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Student_activism" title="Student activism">student activism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robbie_Robertson_(comics)" title="Robbie Robertson (comics)">Robbie Robertson</a>, introduced in <i>The Amazing Spider-Man</i> #51 (Aug. 1967) was one of the first African-American characters in comics to play a serious supporting role.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> In the <i>Fantastic Four</i> series, the lengthy run by Lee and Kirby produced many acclaimed storylines as well as characters that have become central to Marvel, including the <a href="/wiki/Inhumans" title="Inhumans">Inhumans</a><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DeFalco111_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeFalco111-56">[55]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Panther_(comics)" title="Black Panther (comics)">Black Panther</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> an African king who would be mainstream comics' first black superhero.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>The story frequently cited as Lee and Kirby's finest achievement<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> is the three-part "<a href="/wiki/The_Galactus_Trilogy" title="The Galactus Trilogy">Galactus Trilogy</a>" that began in <i>Fantastic Four</i> #48 (March 1966), chronicling the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Galactus" title="Galactus">Galactus</a>, a cosmic giant who wanted to devour the planet, and his herald, the <a href="/wiki/Silver_Surfer" title="Silver Surfer">Silver Surfer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> <i>Fantastic Four</i> #48 was chosen as #24 in the <a href="/wiki/100_Greatest_Marvels_of_All_Time" title="100 Greatest Marvels of All Time">100 Greatest Marvels of All Time</a> poll of Marvel's readers in 2001. Editor <a href="/wiki/Robert_Greenberger" title="Robert Greenberger">Robert Greenberger</a> wrote in his introduction to the story that "As the fourth year of the <i>Fantastic Four</i> came to a close, Stan Lee and Jack Kirby seemed to be only warming up. In retrospect, it was perhaps the most fertile period of any monthly title during the Marvel Age."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Comics historian <a href="/wiki/Les_Daniels" title="Les Daniels">Les Daniels</a> noted that "[t]he mystical and metaphysical elements that took over the saga were perfectly suited to the tastes of young readers in the 1960s", and Lee soon discovered that the story was a favorite on college campuses.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> Lee and artist <a href="/wiki/John_Buscema" title="John Buscema">John Buscema</a> launched <i>The <a href="/wiki/Silver_Surfer_(comic_book)" title="Silver Surfer (comic book)">Silver Surfer</a></i> series in August 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The following year, Lee and <a href="/wiki/Gene_Colan" title="Gene Colan">Gene Colan</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Falcon_(comics)" title="Falcon (comics)">Falcon</a>, comics' first African-American superhero in <i><a href="/wiki/Captain_America_(comic_book)" title="Captain America (comic book)">Captain America</a></i> #117 (Sept. 1969).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> Then in 1971, Lee indirectly helped reform the <a href="/wiki/Comics_Code_Authority" title="Comics Code Authority">Comics Code</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wrightp239_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wrightp239-68">[67]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Health_and_Human_Services" title="United States Department of Health and Human Services">U. S. Department of Health, Education and Welfare</a> had asked Lee to write a comic-book story about the dangers of drugs and Lee conceived a three-issue subplot in <i>The Amazing Spider-Man</i> #96–98 (<a href="/wiki/Cover-date" class="mw-redirect" title="Cover-date">cover-dated</a> May–July 1971), in which Peter Parker's best friend becomes addicted to pills. The Comics Code Authority refused to grant its seal because the stories depicted drug use; the anti-drug context was considered irrelevant. With Goodman's cooperation and confident that the original government request would give him credibility, Lee had the story published without the seal. The comics sold well and Marvel won praise for its socially conscious efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> The CCA subsequently loosened the Code to permit negative depictions of drugs, among other new freedoms.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lee also supported using comic books to provide some measure of social commentary about the real world, often dealing with racism and <a href="/wiki/Bigotry" class="mw-redirect" title="Bigotry">bigotry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> "Stan's Soapbox", besides promoting an upcoming comic book project, also addressed issues of discrimination, intolerance, or prejudice.<sup id="cite_ref-nea_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nea-73">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1972, Lee stopped writing monthly comic books to assume the role of publisher. His final issue of <i>The Amazing Spider-Man</i> was #110 (July 1972)<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> and his last <i>Fantastic Four</i> was #125 (Aug. 1972).<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_career">Later career</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stan_Lee_1975_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Stan_Lee_1975_cropped.jpg/220px-Stan_Lee_1975_cropped.jpg" width="220" height="341" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Stan_Lee_1975_cropped.jpg/330px-Stan_Lee_1975_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Stan_Lee_1975_cropped.jpg/440px-Stan_Lee_1975_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="846" data-file-height="1312" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stan_Lee_1975_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Signed photo of Lee at the 1975 <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Comic-Con_International" class="mw-redirect" title="San Diego Comic-Con International">San Diego Comic-Con</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In later years, Lee became a figurehead and public face for Marvel Comics. He made appearances at comic book conventions around America, lecturing at colleges and participating in panel discussions. Lee and John Romita Sr. launched the Spider-Man newspaper <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man_(comic_strip)" class="mw-redirect" title="Spider-Man (comic strip)">comic strip</a> on January 3, 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> Lee's final collaboration with Jack Kirby, <i>The Silver Surfer: The Ultimate Cosmic Experience</i>, was published in 1978 as part of the <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Fireside_Books" title="Marvel Fireside Books">Marvel Fireside Books</a> series and is considered to be Marvel's first <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">graphic novel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Lee and John Buscema produced the first issue of <i>The Savage <a href="/wiki/She-Hulk" title="She-Hulk">She-Hulk</a></i> (Feb. 1980), which introduced the female cousin of the Hulk<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup> and crafted a Silver Surfer story for <i><a href="/wiki/Epic_Illustrated" title="Epic Illustrated">Epic Illustrated</a></i> #1 (Spring 1980).<sup id="cite_ref-gcd_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gcd-80">[79]</a></sup> He moved to California in 1981 to develop Marvel's TV and movie properties. He has been an executive producer for, and has made <a href="#Film_and_television_appearances">cameo appearances</a> in, Marvel film adaptations and other movies. He occasionally returned to comic book writing with various Silver Surfer projects including a 1982 one-shot drawn by <a href="/wiki/John_Byrne_(comics)" title="John Byrne (comics)">John Byrne</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> the <i>Judgment Day</i> graphic novel illustrated by John Buscema,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> the <i>Parable</i> limited series drawn by French artist <a href="/wiki/Jean_Giraud" title="Jean Giraud">Mœbius</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> and <i>The Enslavers</i> graphic novel with <a href="/wiki/Keith_Pollard" title="Keith Pollard">Keith Pollard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Lee was briefly president of the entire company, but soon stepped down to become publisher instead, finding that being president was too much about numbers and finance and not enough about the creative process he enjoyed.<sup id="cite_ref-LeeMair_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeeMair-85">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Peter_F._Paul" title="Peter F. Paul">Peter Paul</a> and Lee began a new Internet-based <a href="/wiki/Superhero" title="Superhero">superhero</a> creation, production, and marketing studio, <a href="/wiki/Stan_Lee_Media" title="Stan Lee Media">Stan Lee Media</a>, in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-tcj-paul_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tcj-paul-86">[85]</a></sup> It grew to 165 people and went public through a reverse merger structured by investment banker Stan Medley in 1999, but, near the end of 2000, investigators discovered illegal stock manipulation by Paul and corporate officer Stephan Gordon.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> Stan Lee Media filed for <a href="/wiki/Chapter_11_bankruptcy_protection" class="mw-redirect" title="Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection">Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection</a> in February 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> Paul was extradited to the U.S. from Brazil and pleaded guilty to violating <a href="/wiki/SEC_Rule_10b-5" title="SEC Rule 10b-5">SEC Rule 10b-5</a> in connection with trading of his stock in Stan Lee Media.<sup id="cite_ref-USDOJ_2005-03-08_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDOJ_2005-03-08-89">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Washington_Post_2005-10-09_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Washington_Post_2005-10-09-90">[89]</a></sup> Lee was never implicated in the scheme. In 2001, Lee, Gill Champion, and Arthur Lieberman formed <a href="/wiki/POW!_Entertainment" title="POW! Entertainment">POW! (Purveyors of Wonder) Entertainment</a> to develop film, television and video game properties. Lee created the risqué animated superhero series <i><a href="/wiki/Stripperella" title="Stripperella">Stripperella</a></i> for <a href="/wiki/Spike_TV" class="mw-redirect" title="Spike TV">Spike TV</a>. In 2004 POW! Entertainment went public via another reverse merger, structured again by investment banker Stan Medley. Also in 2004, Lee announced a superhero program that would feature <a href="/wiki/Ringo_Starr" title="Ringo Starr">Ringo Starr</a>, the former <a href="/wiki/Beatles" class="mw-redirect" title="Beatles">Beatle</a>, as the lead character.<sup id="cite_ref-ringo_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ringo-91">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> Additionally, in August of that year, Lee announced the launch of Stan Lee's Sunday Comics,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> a short-lived subscription service hosted by <a href="/wiki/Komikwerks.com" class="mw-redirect" title="Komikwerks.com">Komikwerks.com</a>. On March 15, 2007, after Stan Lee Media had been purchased by Jim Nesfield, the company filed a lawsuit against <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Entertainment" title="Marvel Entertainment">Marvel Entertainment</a> for $5&#160;billion, claiming Lee had given his rights to several Marvel characters to Stan Lee Media in exchange for stock and a salary.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> On June 9, 2007, Stan Lee Media sued Lee; his newer company, POW! Entertainment; and POW! subsidiary QED Entertainment.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2008, Lee wrote humorous captions for the political <a href="/wiki/Photo_strip" class="mw-redirect" title="Photo strip">fumetti</a> book <i>Stan Lee Presents Election Daze: What Are They Really Saying?</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> In April of that year, Brighton Partners and <a href="/wiki/Rainmaker_Animation" class="mw-redirect" title="Rainmaker Animation">Rainmaker Animation</a> announced a partnership POW! to produce a CGI film series, <i>Legion of 5</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup> Other projects by Lee announced in the late 2000s included a line of superhero comics for <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Comics" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Comics">Virgin Comics</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> a TV adaptation of the novel <i><a href="/wiki/Hero_(novel)" title="Hero (novel)">Hero</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup> a foreword to <i><a href="/wiki/Skyscraperman" class="mw-redirect" title="Skyscraperman">Skyscraperman</a></i> by skyscraper fire-safety advocate and Spider-Man fan <a href="/wiki/Dan_Goodwin" title="Dan Goodwin">Dan Goodwin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup> a partnership with <a href="/wiki/Guardian_Media_Entertainment" title="Guardian Media Entertainment">Guardian Media Entertainment</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Guardian_Project_(comics)" title="The Guardian Project (comics)">The Guardian Project</a> to create <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">NHL</a> superhero mascots<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> and work with the Eagle Initiative program to find new talent in the comic book field.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:10.14.11StanLeeByLuigiNovi9.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/10.14.11StanLeeByLuigiNovi9.jpg/220px-10.14.11StanLeeByLuigiNovi9.jpg" width="220" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/10.14.11StanLeeByLuigiNovi9.jpg/330px-10.14.11StanLeeByLuigiNovi9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/10.14.11StanLeeByLuigiNovi9.jpg/440px-10.14.11StanLeeByLuigiNovi9.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1507" data-file-height="1409" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:10.14.11StanLeeByLuigiNovi9.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lee promoting Stan Lee's Kids Universe at the 2011 <a href="/wiki/New_York_Comic_Con" title="New York Comic Con">New York Comic Con</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In October, Lee announced he would partner with 1821 Comics on a multimedia imprint for children, Stan Lee's Kids Universe, a move he said addressed the lack of comic books targeted for that demographic; and that he was collaborating with the company on its futuristic graphic novel <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_%26_Juliet" class="mw-redirect" title="Romeo &amp; Juliet">Romeo &amp; Juliet</a>: The War</i>, by writer Max Work and artist Skan Srisuwan.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> At the 2012 San Diego Comic-Con International, Lee announced his YouTube channel, <i><a href="/wiki/Stan_Lee%27s_World_of_Heroes" title="Stan Lee's World of Heroes">Stan Lee's World of Heroes</a></i>, which airs programs created by Lee, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hamill" title="Mark Hamill">Mark Hamill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_David" title="Peter David">Peter David</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adrianne_Curry" title="Adrianne Curry">Adrianne Curry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bonnie_Burton" title="Bonnie Burton">Bonnie Burton</a> among others.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> Lee wrote the book, <i>Zodiac</i> released in January 2015, with <a href="/wiki/Stuart_Moore" title="Stuart Moore">Stuart Moore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> The film <i>Stan Lee's Annihilator</i>, based on a Chinese prisoner-turned-superhero named Ming and in production since 2013, is set for a 2015 release.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his later career, Lee's contributions continued to expand outside the style that he helped pioneer. An example of this is his first work for DC Comics in the 2000s, launching the <i><a href="/wiki/Just_Imagine..." title="Just Imagine...">Just Imagine...</a></i> series, in which Lee re-imagined the DC superheroes <a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">Superman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">Batman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wonder_Woman" title="Wonder Woman">Wonder Woman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_Lantern" title="Green Lantern">Green Lantern</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Flash_(comics)" title="Flash (comics)">Flash</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">Manga</a> projects involving Lee include <i><a href="/wiki/Karakurid%C3%B4ji_Ultimo" class="mw-redirect" title="Karakuridôji Ultimo">Karakuridôji Ultimo</a></i>, a collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Hiroyuki_Takei" title="Hiroyuki Takei">Hiroyuki Takei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viz_Media" title="Viz Media">Viz Media</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shueisha" title="Shueisha">Shueisha</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Heroman" title="Heroman">Heroman</a></i>, serialized in <a href="/wiki/Square_Enix" title="Square Enix">Square Enix</a>'s <i>Monthly Shōnen Gangan</i> with the Japanese company <a href="/wiki/Bones_(studio)" title="Bones (studio)">Bones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> In 2011, Lee started writing a live-action musical, <i>The Yin and Yang Battle of Tao</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-playb_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-playb-118">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>This period also saw a number of collaborators honor Lee for his influence on the comics industry. In 2006, Marvel commemorated Lee's 65 years with the company by publishing a series of one-shot comics starring Lee himself meeting and interacting with many of his co-creations, including Spider-Man, Doctor Strange, the <a href="/wiki/Thing_(comics)" title="Thing (comics)">Thing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver_Surfer" title="Silver Surfer">Silver Surfer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Doom" title="Doctor Doom">Doctor Doom</a>. These comics also featured short pieces by such comics creators as <a href="/wiki/Joss_Whedon" title="Joss Whedon">Joss Whedon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fred_Hembeck" title="Fred Hembeck">Fred Hembeck</a>, as well as reprints of classic Lee-written adventures.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards8242006_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards8242006-119">[118]</a></sup> At the 2007 <a href="/wiki/Comic-Con_International" class="mw-redirect" title="Comic-Con International">Comic-Con International</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Legends" title="Marvel Legends">Marvel Legends</a> introduced a Stan Lee <a href="/wiki/Action_figure" title="Action figure">action figure</a>. The body beneath the figure's removable cloth wardrobe is a re-used mold of a previously released Spider-Man action figure, with minor changes.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Comikaze_Expo" class="mw-redirect" title="Comikaze Expo">Comikaze Expo</a>, Los Angeles' largest comic book convention, was rebranded as Stan Lee's Comikaze Presented by <a href="/wiki/POW!_Entertainment" title="POW! Entertainment">POW! Entertainment</a> in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the 2016 <a href="/wiki/Comic-Con_International" class="mw-redirect" title="Comic-Con International">Comic-Con International</a>, Lee introduced his digital graphic novel <i>Stan Lee's 'God Woke'</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-eclipsemagazine_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eclipsemagazine-122">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cctvamerica_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cctvamerica-124">[123]</a></sup> with text originally written as a poem he presented at <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Hall" title="Carnegie Hall">Carnegie Hall</a> in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-conversations_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-conversations-125">[124]</a></sup> The print-book version won the 2017 <a href="/wiki/Independent_Publisher_Book_Awards" title="Independent Publisher Book Awards">Independent Publisher Book Awards</a>' Outstanding Books of the Year Independent Voice Award.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Charity_work">Charity work</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Stan_Lee_Foundation" title="Stan Lee Foundation">Stan Lee Foundation</a> was founded in 2010 to focus on literacy, education, and the arts. Its stated goals include supporting programs and ideas that improve access to literacy resources, as well as promoting diversity, national literacy, culture and the arts.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>Stan Lee has donated portions of his personal effects to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wyoming" title="University of Wyoming">University of Wyoming</a> at various times, between 1981 and 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-Stan_Lee_Papers_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stan_Lee_Papers-128">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fictional_portrayals">Fictional portrayals</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_comics_creators_appearing_in_comics" title="List of comics creators appearing in comics">List of comics creators appearing in comics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FF10.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/FF10.jpg/240px-FF10.jpg" width="240" height="362" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/FF10.jpg/360px-FF10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/fd/FF10.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="603" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FF10.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lee and Kirby (bottom left) as themselves on the cover of <i><a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four" title="Fantastic Four">The Fantastic Four</a></i> #10 (Jan. 1963). Art by Kirby and <a href="/wiki/Dick_Ayers" title="Dick Ayers">Dick Ayers</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Stan Lee and his collaborator <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kirby" title="Jack Kirby">Jack Kirby</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_comics_creators_appearing_in_comics" title="List of comics creators appearing in comics">appear as themselves</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four" title="Fantastic Four">The Fantastic Four</a></i> #10 (Jan. 1963), the first of several appearances within the fictional <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Universe" title="Marvel Universe">Marvel Universe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> The two are depicted as similar to their real-world counterparts, creating comic books based on the "real" adventures of the Fantastic Four.</p>
<p>Lee was parodied by Kirby in comics published by rival <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">DC Comics</a> as <a href="/wiki/Funky_Flashman" title="Funky Flashman">Funky Flashman</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> Kirby later portrayed himself, Lee, production executive <a href="/wiki/Sol_Brodsky" title="Sol Brodsky">Sol Brodsky</a>, and Lee's secretary <a href="/wiki/Flo_Steinberg" title="Flo Steinberg">Flo Steinberg</a> as superheroes in <i><a href="/wiki/What_If_(comics)" title="What If (comics)">What If</a></i> #11 (Oct. 1978), "What If the Marvel Bullpen Had Become the Fantastic Four?", in which Lee played the part of <a href="/wiki/Mister_Fantastic" title="Mister Fantastic">Mister Fantastic</a>. Lee has also made numerous cameo appearances in many Marvel titles, appearing in audiences and crowds at many characters' ceremonies and parties, and hosting an old-soldiers reunion in <i><a href="/wiki/Sgt._Fury_and_his_Howling_Commandos" title="Sgt. Fury and his Howling Commandos">Sgt. Fury and his Howling Commandos</a></i> #100 (July 1972). Lee appeared, unnamed, as the priest at <a href="/wiki/Luke_Cage" title="Luke Cage">Luke Cage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jessica_Jones" title="Jessica Jones">Jessica Jones</a>' wedding in <i>New Avengers Annual</i> #1 (June 2006). He pays his respects to <a href="/wiki/Karen_Page" title="Karen Page">Karen Page</a> at her funeral in <i><a href="/wiki/Daredevil_(Marvel_Comics_series)" title="Daredevil (Marvel Comics series)">Daredevil</a></i> vol. 2, #8 (June 1998), and appears in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Spider-Man" title="The Amazing Spider-Man">The Amazing Spider-Man</a></i> #169 (June 1977).</p>
<p>In 1994, artist <a href="/wiki/Alex_Ross" title="Alex Ross">Alex Ross</a> rendered Lee as a bar patron on page 44 of <i><a href="/wiki/Marvels" title="Marvels">Marvels</a></i> #3.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Marvel's "<a href="/wiki/Flashback_(comics)" title="Flashback (comics)">Flashback</a>" series of titles <a href="/wiki/Cover-date" class="mw-redirect" title="Cover-date">cover-dated</a> July 1997, a top-hatted caricature of Lee as a <a href="/wiki/Ringmaster_(circus)" title="Ringmaster (circus)">ringmaster</a> introduced stories that detailed events in Marvel characters' lives before they became superheroes, in special "-1" editions of many Marvel titles. The "ringmaster" depiction of Lee was originally from <i><a href="/wiki/Generation_X_(comics)" title="Generation X (comics)">Generation X</a></i> #17 (July 1996), where the character narrated a story set primarily in an abandoned circus. Though the story itself was written by <a href="/wiki/Scott_Lobdell" title="Scott Lobdell">Scott Lobdell</a>, the narration by "Ringmaster Stan" was written by Lee, and the character was drawn in that issue by <a href="/wiki/Chris_Bachalo" title="Chris Bachalo">Chris Bachalo</a>.</p>
<p>Lee and other comics creators are mentioned on page 479 of <a href="/wiki/Michael_Chabon" title="Michael Chabon">Michael Chabon</a>'s 2000 novel about the comics industry <i><a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Adventures_of_Kavalier_and_Clay" class="mw-redirect" title="The Amazing Adventures of Kavalier and Clay">The Amazing Adventures of Kavalier and Clay</a></i>. Chabon also acknowledges a debt to Lee and other creators on the book's Author's Note page.</p>
<p>On one of the last pages of <i><a href="/wiki/Truth:_Red,_White_%26_Black" title="Truth: Red, White &amp; Black">Truth: Red, White &amp; Black</a></i>, Lee appears in a real photograph among other celebrities on a wall of the Bradley home.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> Under his given name of Stanley Lieber, Stan Lee appears briefly in <a href="/wiki/Paul_Malmont" title="Paul Malmont">Paul Malmont</a>'s 2006 novel <i>The Chinatown Death Cloud Peril</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Stan_Lee_Meets_Superheroes" title="Stan Lee Meets Superheroes">Stan Lee Meets Superheroes</a></i>, which Lee wrote, he comes into contact with some of his favorite creations.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards8242006_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards8242006-119">[118]</a></sup> Stan Lee and Jack Kirby appear as professors in <i><a href="/wiki/Marvel_Adventures_Spider-Man" title="Marvel Adventures Spider-Man">Marvel Adventures Spider-Man</a></i> #19.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Lavie_Tidhar" title="Lavie Tidhar">Lavie Tidhar</a>'s 2013 <i>The Violent Century</i>, Lee appears – under his birth name of "Stanley Martin Lieber" – as a historian of superhumans.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_and_television_appearances">Film and television appearances</span></h2>
<p>Lee has had cameo appearances in many Marvel film and television projects. A few of these appearances are self-aware and sometimes reference Lee's involvement in the creation of certain characters. Lee has been a credited <a href="/wiki/Executive_producer" title="Executive producer">executive producer</a> on most Marvel film and television projects since the <a href="/wiki/Captain_America_(1990_film)" title="Captain America (1990 film)">1990 direct-to-video Captain America</a> film.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marvel_television">Marvel television</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Animation">Animation</span></h4>
<ul>
<li>One of Lee's earliest contributions to animation based on Marvel properties was narrating the <a href="/wiki/The_Incredible_Hulk_(1982_animated_TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Incredible Hulk (1982 animated TV series)">1980s <i>Incredible Hulk</i> animated series</a>, always beginning his narration with a self-introduction and ending with "This is Stan Lee saying, <i>Excelsior!"</i> Lee had previously narrated the "Seven Little Superheroes" episode of <i><a href="/wiki/Spider-Man_and_His_Amazing_Friends" title="Spider-Man and His Amazing Friends">Spider-Man and His Amazing Friends</a>,</i> which the <i>Hulk</i> series was paired with for broadcast.</li>
<li>Lee did the narration for the original 1989 <a href="/wiki/X-Men" title="X-Men">X-Men</a> animated series pilot titled <i><a href="/wiki/X-Men:_Pryde_of_the_X-Men" title="X-Men: Pryde of the X-Men">X-Men: Pryde of the X-Men</a>.</i></li>
<li>Lee was an executive producer of the 1990s animated TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Spider-Man_(1994_TV_series)" title="Spider-Man (1994 TV series)">Spider-Man</a>.</i> He appeared as himself in animated form in the final episode titled "<a href="/wiki/Spider_Wars#Spider_Wars.2C_Chapter_II:_.22Farewell.2C_Spider-Man.22" class="mw-redirect" title="Spider Wars">Farewell, Spider-Man</a>". Spider-Man is transported by <a href="/wiki/Madame_Web" title="Madame Web">Madame Web</a> into the "real" world where he is a fictional character. He meets Lee and the two swing around until Spider-Man drops him off on top of a building; Madame Web appears and brings Spider-Man back to his homeworld. Realizing he is stuck on a roof, Lee muses, hoping the Fantastic Four will show up and lend a hand.</li>
<li>He also voices the character "Frank Elson" in an episode of <i><a href="/wiki/Spider-Man:_The_New_Animated_Series" title="Spider-Man: The New Animated Series">Spider-Man: The New Animated Series</a></i> broadcast by MTV in 2003, titled "Mind Games" (Parts 1 and 2, originally aired on August 15 and 22, 2003).</li>
<li>He voiced a loading-dock worker named Stan on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Spectacular_Spider-Man_(TV_series)" title="The Spectacular Spider-Man (TV series)">The Spectacular Spider-Man</a></i> in the episode "<a href="/wiki/List_of_Spectacular_Spider-Man_episodes#Season_2" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Spectacular Spider-Man episodes">Blueprints</a>".</li>
<li>In several episodes of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Super_Hero_Squad_Show" title="The Super Hero Squad Show">The Super Hero Squad Show</a>,</i> Lee voices the Mayor of Super Hero City.</li>
<li>Lee has appeared in episodes of the <a href="/wiki/Disney_XD" title="Disney XD">Disney XD</a> TV series <i><a href="/wiki/Ultimate_Spider-Man_(TV_series)" title="Ultimate Spider-Man (TV series)">Ultimate Spider-Man</a></i> as a high school janitor named Stan, in which he makes references to Lee's real-life career. In the pilot "Great Power" and the episode "Why I Hate Gym", he mentions <a href="/wiki/Irving_Forbush" class="mw-redirect" title="Irving Forbush">Irving Forbush</a>, an in-joke character Lee co-created in 1955 as a literary device.<sup id="cite_ref-Why_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Why-135">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Why_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Why-135">[134]</a></sup> Stan the Janitor also appears in Episode 18, "Out of Damage Control", as a part-time worker for <a href="/wiki/Damage_Control_(comics)" title="Damage Control (comics)">Damage Control</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup> In the episode "Stan By Me", he, along with <a href="/wiki/Mary_Jane_Watson" title="Mary Jane Watson">Mary Jane Watson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phil_Coulson" title="Phil Coulson">Agent/Principal Coulson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Osborn" title="Harry Osborn">Harry Osborn</a>, helped <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man" title="Spider-Man">Spider-Man</a> fight the <a href="/wiki/Lizard_(comics)" title="Lizard (comics)">Lizard</a>. At the end of the episode it is revealed that Lee's character is secretly a top <a href="/wiki/S.H.I.E.L.D." title="S.H.I.E.L.D.">S.H.I.E.L.D.</a> agent and is aware of Peter Parker's secret identity as Spider-Man,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> and that he is a founding member of S.H.I.E.L.D. who named the organization, which Lee named in reality.<sup id="cite_ref-IGNJan2014_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGNJan2014-138">[137]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Live-action">Live-action</span></h4>
<ul>
<li>In the <a href="/wiki/TV-movie" class="mw-redirect" title="TV-movie">TV-movie</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Trial_of_the_Incredible_Hulk" title="The Trial of the Incredible Hulk">The Trial of the Incredible Hulk</a></i> (1989), Lee's first appearance in a Marvel movie or TV project is as a jury foreman in the trial of <a href="/wiki/Hulk_(comics)" title="Hulk (comics)">Dr. David Banner</a>.</li>
<li>Lee appears in "<a href="/wiki/T.R.A.C.K.S." title="T.R.A.C.K.S.">T.R.A.C.K.S.</a>", an episode of <i><a href="/wiki/Agents_of_S.H.I.E.L.D." title="Agents of S.H.I.E.L.D.">Agents of S.H.I.E.L.D.</a></i> originally broadcast February 4, 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup></li>
<li>He appears in "<a href="/wiki/The_Blitzkrieg_Button" title="The Blitzkrieg Button">The Blitzkrieg Button</a>", the <i><a href="/wiki/Agent_Carter_(TV_series)" title="Agent Carter (TV series)">Agent Carter</a></i> episode originally broadcast January 27, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lee cameos in <a href="/wiki/List_of_Marvel_Cinematic_Universe_television_series#Netflix_series" title="List of Marvel Cinematic Universe television series">Marvel's Netflix series</a> via a photograph of Lee as a police officer, appearing in <i><a href="/wiki/Daredevil_(TV_series)" title="Daredevil (TV series)">Daredevil</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Jessica_Jones_(TV_series)" title="Jessica Jones (TV series)">Jessica Jones</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Luke_Cage_(TV_series)" title="Luke Cage (TV series)">Luke Cage</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Iron_Fist_(TV_series)" title="Iron Fist (TV series)">Iron Fist</a></i>. In <i>Iron Fist</i>, he is identified as Captain Irving <a href="/wiki/Forbush_Man" title="Forbush Man">Forbush</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marvel_films">Marvel films</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Animation_2">Animation</span></h4>
<ul>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Big_Hero_6_(film)" title="Big Hero 6 (film)">Big Hero 6</a></i> (2014), Lee's voice and likeness are used for the father of character <a href="/wiki/Fredzilla" title="Fredzilla">Fred</a>. Though he appears in a portrait earlier in the film, Lee's cameo is a post-credits scene in which he demonstrates to his son that they have many things to talk about. Lee was not involved with the creation of <a href="/wiki/Big_Hero_6_(comics)" title="Big Hero 6 (comics)">Big Hero 6</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Live-action_2">Live-action</span></h4>
<ul>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/X-Men_(film)" title="X-Men (film)"><i>X-Men</i></a> (2000), Lee appears as a hotdog stand vendor on the beach when the newly mutated <a href="/wiki/Senator_Kelly" class="mw-redirect" title="Senator Kelly">Senator Kelly</a> emerges naked onshore after escaping from <a href="/wiki/Magneto_(comics)" title="Magneto (comics)">Magneto</a>.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man_(2002_film)" title="Spider-Man (2002 film)"><i>Spider-Man</i></a> (2002), he appeared during <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man" title="Spider-Man">Spider-Man</a>'s first battle with <a href="/wiki/Green_Goblin" title="Green Goblin">the Green Goblin</a>, pulling a little girl away from falling debris. In the DVD's deleted scenes, Lee plays a street vendor who tries to sell Peter Parker a pair of sunglasses.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Daredevil_(film)" title="Daredevil (film)"><i>Daredevil</i></a> (2003), as a child, <a href="/wiki/Daredevil_(Marvel_Comics_character)" title="Daredevil (Marvel Comics character)">Matt Murdock</a> stops Lee from crossing the street and getting hit by a bus. This is also a reference to an incident in the comics which was omitted from the origin story of the film.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Hulk_(film)" title="Hulk (film)"><i>Hulk</i></a> (2003), he appears walking alongside former TV-series Hulk <a href="/wiki/Lou_Ferrigno" title="Lou Ferrigno">Lou Ferrigno</a> in an early scene, both as security guards at Bruce Banner's lab. It was his first speaking role in a film based on one of his characters.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Spider-Man_2" title="Spider-Man 2">Spider-Man 2</a></i> (2004), Lee pulls an innocent person away from danger during Spider-Man's first battle with <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Octopus" title="Doctor Octopus">Doctor Octopus</a>. In a blooper scene that appears as an extra on the film's DVD release, Lee has another cameo, saying, "Look, Spider-Man stole that kid's sneakers." Stan Lee also appears talking to Peter Parker.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Man-Thing_(film)" title="Man-Thing (film)"><i>Man-Thing</i></a> (2005), a photograph of Lee is seen on a board of missing people.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fantastic1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f1/Fantastic1.jpg/280px-Fantastic1.jpg" width="280" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f1/Fantastic1.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="259" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fantastic1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lee as <a href="/wiki/Willie_Lumpkin" title="Willie Lumpkin">Willie Lumpkin</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four_(2005_film)" title="Fantastic Four (2005 film)">Fantastic Four</a></i>, 2005.</div>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four_(2005_film)" title="Fantastic Four (2005 film)"><i>Fantastic Four</i></a> (2005), Lee appears for the first time as a character that he created for the comics, <a href="/wiki/Willie_Lumpkin" title="Willie Lumpkin">Willie Lumpkin</a>, the mail carrier who greets the <a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four" title="Fantastic Four">Fantastic Four</a> as they enter the <a href="/wiki/Baxter_Building" title="Baxter Building">Baxter Building</a>.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/X-Men:_The_Last_Stand" title="X-Men: The Last Stand">X-Men: The Last Stand</a></i> (2006), Lee and <a href="/wiki/Chris_Claremont" title="Chris Claremont">Chris Claremont</a> appear as two of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Grey" title="Jean Grey">Jean Grey</a>'s neighbors in the opening scenes set 20 years in the past. Lee, credited as "Waterhose Man", is watering the lawn when Jean telekinetically redirects the water from the hose into the air.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four:_Rise_of_the_Silver_Surfer" title="Fantastic Four: Rise of the Silver Surfer">Fantastic Four: Rise of the Silver Surfer</a></i> (2007), Lee appears as himself at Reed Richards' and Susan Storm's first wedding, being turned away by a security guard for not being on the guest list. In <i>Fantastic Four Annual</i> #3 (1965), in which the couple married, Lee and <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kirby" title="Jack Kirby">Jack Kirby</a> are similarly turned away.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Spider-Man_3" title="Spider-Man 3">Spider-Man 3</a></i> (2007), Lee appears in a credited role as "Man in Times Square". He stands next to Peter Parker, both of them reading a news bulletin about Spider-Man, and commenting to Peter that, "You know, I guess one person can make a difference". He then says his <a href="/wiki/Catchphrase" title="Catchphrase">catchphrase</a>, "'Nuff said" and leaves Peter to dwell on that thought.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Iron_Man_(2008_film)" title="Iron Man (2008 film)">Iron Man</a></i> (2008), Lee, credited as "Himself", appears at a gala cavorting with three blondes, where Tony Stark mistakes him for <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Hefner" title="Hugh Hefner">Hugh Hefner</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> In the theatrical release of the film, Stark simply greets Lee as "Hef" and moves on; another version of the scene was filmed where Stark realizes his mistake, but Lee graciously responds, "That's okay, I get this all the time."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup> In 2008, Stan Lee named this as his favorite cameo appearance to that date.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Incredible_Hulk_(film)" title="The Incredible Hulk (film)">The Incredible Hulk</a></i> (2008), Lee appears as a hapless citizen who accidentally ingests a <a href="/wiki/Soft_drink" title="Soft drink">soft drink</a> mixed with Bruce Banner's blood, subsequently dropping it and leading to the discovery of Dr. Banner's location in a bottling plant in Brazil.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Iron_Man_2" title="Iron Man 2">Iron Man 2</a></i> (2010), during the Stark Expo, Lee, wearing suspenders and a red shirt and black and purple tie, is mistakenly greeted by Tony Stark as "<a href="/wiki/Larry_King" title="Larry King">Larry King</a>".</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Thor_(film)" title="Thor (film)">Thor</a></i> (2011), Lee appears among many people at the site where <a href="/wiki/Thor_(Marvel_Comics)" title="Thor (Marvel Comics)">Thor</a>'s hammer <a href="/wiki/Mjolnir_(comics)" title="Mjolnir (comics)">Mjolnir</a> lands on earth. He tears the bed off his <a href="/wiki/Pickup_truck" title="Pickup truck">pickup truck</a> in an attempt to pull Mjolnir out of the ground with a chain and causes everyone in the scene to laugh by asking, "Did it work?". His character is credited as "Stan the Man", a nickname he'd adopted in the Silver Age of Comic Books.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Captain_America:_The_First_Avenger" title="Captain America: The First Avenger">Captain America: The First Avenger</a></i> (2011), Lee plays a general in World War II who mistakes another man for Captain America/Steve Rogers, commenting, "I thought he'd be taller." Lee had nothing to do with the basic creation of the title character. However, Lee began his writing career in the character's original series where he created the idea of Captain America using his shield as a throwing weapon. Furthermore, he was responsible for reviving the character in the <a href="/wiki/Silver_Age_of_Comic_Books" title="Silver Age of Comic Books">Silver Age of Comic Books</a> and co-wrote most of the character's stories in <i><a href="/wiki/Avengers_(comics)" title="Avengers (comics)">The Avengers</a></i> and his solo stories in that period.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Avengers_(2012_film)" title="The Avengers (2012 film)">The Avengers</a></i> (2012), Lee's character is interviewed about the Avengers saving Manhattan. Lee's character responds, "Superheroes in New York? Give me a break", and then returns to his game of chess.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup> He also appears in a deleted scene in which, when a waitress flirts with Steve Rogers, Lee says to him, "Ask for her number, you moron!"<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Spider-Man_(2012_film)" title="The Amazing Spider-Man (2012 film)">The Amazing Spider-Man</a></i> (2012), Lee is a librarian at Midtown Science High School, comically oblivious to the fight between Spider-Man and the Lizard happening behind him (a table nearly hits him as well) due to the fact that he is listening to classical music. He walks out of the library as the fight continues.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Iron_Man_3" title="Iron Man 3">Iron Man 3</a></i> (2013), Lee portrays a beauty pageant judge who appears on a television monitor and happily gives one of the contestants a 10.<sup id="cite_ref-StanLee_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StanLee-152">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Thor:_The_Dark_World" title="Thor: The Dark World">Thor: The Dark World</a></i> (2013), Lee appears as a mental ward patient who loans his shoe to <a href="/wiki/Erik_Selvig" title="Erik Selvig">Erik Selvig</a> for a demonstration about "the Convergence" in his delusions. When Selvig finishes and asks if anyone has questions, Lee says, "Yeah, can I have my shoe back"?<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Captain_America:_The_Winter_Soldier" title="Captain America: The Winter Soldier">Captain America: The Winter Soldier</a></i> (2014), Lee plays a security guard at the <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">Smithsonian Institution</a> who after discovering that Captain America stole his own World War II uniform from an exhibit, says, "Oh man, I am <i>so</i> fired."</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Spider-Man_2" title="The Amazing Spider-Man 2">The Amazing Spider-Man 2</a></i> (2014), Lee is a guest at Peter Parker and Gwen Stacy's graduation. He notes that he recognizes Peter Parker.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Guardians_of_the_Galaxy_(film)" title="Guardians of the Galaxy (film)">Guardians of the Galaxy</a></i> (2014), Lee appears as an elderly gentleman having a conversation with a significantly younger woman. <a href="/wiki/Rocket_Raccoon" title="Rocket Raccoon">Rocket</a>, viewing him through a scanning device, dismisses him as part of what he saw was wrong with the planet Xandar. With the exception of <a href="/wiki/Groot" title="Groot">Groot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ronan_the_Accuser" title="Ronan the Accuser">Ronan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Collector_(comics)" title="Collector (comics)">The Collector</a>, Lee did not have a hand in the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Guardians_of_the_Galaxy_(2008_team)" title="Guardians of the Galaxy (2008 team)">Guardians of the Galaxy</a>.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Avengers:_Age_of_Ultron" title="Avengers: Age of Ultron">Avengers: Age of Ultron</a></i> (2015), he appears as a military veteran who attends the Avengers' victory party. He claims he fought at <a href="/wiki/Omaha_Beach" title="Omaha Beach">Omaha Beach</a> and that it proves he can handle a shot of <a href="/wiki/Asgard_(comics)" title="Asgard (comics)">Asgardian</a> liquor from Thor, but is then carried away drunk, muttering his catchphrase, "Excelsior!"<sup id="cite_ref-Lee_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lee-154">[153]</a></sup> Lee noted that this was his favorite cameo.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Ant-Man_(film)" title="Ant-Man (film)">Ant-Man</a></i> (2015), Lee appears as a bartender in a story Luis tells to <a href="/wiki/Scott_Lang" class="mw-redirect" title="Scott Lang">Scott Lang</a> explaining that <a href="/wiki/Falcon_(comics)" title="Falcon (comics)">Falcon</a> was looking for him.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup></li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Deadpool_(film)" title="Deadpool (film)">Deadpool</a></i> (2016), Lee appears as himself, working as an MC at a strip club.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> Lee did not have a hand in the creation of the character <a href="/wiki/Deadpool" title="Deadpool">Deadpool</a>.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Captain_America:_Civil_War" title="Captain America: Civil War">Captain America: Civil War</a></i> (2016), Lee appears as a <a href="/wiki/FedEx" title="FedEx">FedEx</a> postman, delivering a package from Steve Rogers to Tony Stark at the end of the film, mispronouncing Stark's name as Tony "Stank".</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/X-Men:_Apocalypse" title="X-Men: Apocalypse">X-Men: Apocalypse</a></i> (2016), Lee is shown viewing the rising nuclear weapons launched by <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse_(comics)" title="Apocalypse (comics)">Apocalypse</a> next to his wife Joan.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Strange_(film)" title="Doctor Strange (film)">Doctor Strange</a></i> (2016), Lee appears as a bus rider reading <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Doors_of_Perception" title="The Doors of Perception">The Doors of Perception</a></i>, oblivious to a battle between <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Strange" title="Doctor Strange">Doctor Strange</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baron_Mordo" title="Baron Mordo">Mordo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kaecilius" class="mw-redirect" title="Kaecilius">Kaecilius</a> and his followers.<sup id="cite_ref-Buxton_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buxton-158">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Buxton_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buxton-158">[157]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lee makes a cameo appearance in <i><a href="/wiki/Deadpool:_No_Good_Deed" class="mw-redirect" title="Deadpool: No Good Deed">Deadpool: No Good Deed</a></i> (2017), a short film aired before <i><a href="/wiki/Logan_(film)" title="Logan (film)">Logan</a></i> in North America, in which Lee plays himself and says to Deadpool "Wow nice suit!" to which Deadpool replies "Zip it Stan Lee!"<sup id="cite_ref-Reynolds-Deadpool-2-Lee_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reynolds-Deadpool-2-Lee-159">[158]</a></sup> Lee's cameo appearance was cut from the theatrical version of the short film, but was restored in the version released on star <a href="/wiki/Ryan_Reynolds" title="Ryan Reynolds">Ryan Reynolds</a>' <a href="/wiki/Youtube" class="mw-redirect" title="Youtube">Youtube</a> account page. Lee later thanked Reynolds for restoring his cameo.</li>
<li>Lee will make a cameo appearance in <i><a href="/wiki/Guardians_of_the_Galaxy_Vol._2" title="Guardians of the Galaxy Vol. 2">Guardians of the Galaxy Vol. 2</a></i> (2017).<sup id="cite_ref-LeeCameo_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeeCameo-160">[159]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lee will make a cameo appearance in <i><a href="/wiki/Spider-Man:_Homecoming" title="Spider-Man: Homecoming">Spider-Man: Homecoming</a></i> (2017).<sup id="cite_ref-LeeCameo_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeeCameo-160">[159]</a></sup></li>
<li>Lee will make a cameo appearance in <i><a href="/wiki/Deadpool_2" class="mw-redirect" title="Deadpool 2">Deadpool 2</a></i> (2018).</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warner_Bros..2FDC_properties">Warner Bros./DC properties</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stan_lee_on_stas_apokolips_now_part2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cd/Stan_lee_on_stas_apokolips_now_part2.jpg/220px-Stan_lee_on_stas_apokolips_now_part2.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/c/cd/Stan_lee_on_stas_apokolips_now_part2.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stan_lee_on_stas_apokolips_now_part2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stan Lee mourning on Dan Turpin's funeral. Above TV capture from original episode and below storyboard art by Bruce Timm and text comments by Paul Dini.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the original February 7, 1998, broadcast airing of the <i><a href="/wiki/Superman:_The_Animated_Series" title="Superman: The Animated Series">Superman: The Animated Series</a></i> episode "Apokolips... Now! Part 2" on the <a href="/wiki/Kids%27_WB" title="Kids' WB">Kids' WB</a> programming block, an animated Stan Lee was visible mourning the death of <a href="/wiki/Dan_Turpin" title="Dan Turpin">Daniel "Terrible" Turpin</a>, a character based on his longtime <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">Marvel Comics</a> collaborator <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kirby" title="Jack Kirby">Jack Kirby</a>. This shot was later modified to remove the likeness of Lee and other of background Marvel characters when the episode was released on DVD.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_film.2C_TV.2C_and_video">Other film, TV, and video</span></h3>
<ul>
<li>In the 1990s, Lee hosted the documentary series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Comic_Book_Greats" title="The Comic Book Greats">The Comic Book Greats</a></i> and interviewed notable comic book creators such as <a href="/wiki/Chris_Claremont" title="Chris Claremont">Chris Claremont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jim_Lee" title="Jim Lee">Jim Lee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Todd_McFarlane" title="Todd McFarlane">Todd McFarlane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rob_Liefeld" title="Rob Liefeld">Rob Liefeld</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Whilce_Portacio" title="Whilce Portacio">Whilce Portacio</a>.</li>
